0618680004|5.0|I read many reviews (and comments posted on Amazon) about this book which are absolute nonsense.  You might read that this book is sour, excessive, virulent and full of anti-religious zeal.  This is plainly false, since most of the tone of this book is actually the mixture of plain language and factual scholarship which is usual in Dawkins' essays (which you should read whatever your religious beliefs!).  Certain sections do have an emotional and indignant tone -- but I assume that any reader, believer or not, would be as indignant as Dawkins is when writing about some absolute religious horrors, like sectarian child abuse and indoctrination. <br />The initial half of the book is devoted to a scientific examination of the possible existence of God.  Arguments and counter-arguments for the existence of God have been discussed for centuries, but Dawkins' summary is excellent and up-to-date.  An intermediate series of chapters deals with the origin of religion and religious belief.  Here, Dawkins adopts his own, sharp, evolutionary view on this topic, and comes out with arguments and explanation far more elaborate and convincing than you might read in other, similar books (far superior, in my opinion, to Dennett's \\"Breaking the Spell\\").  The last chapters deal with religion and ethics, and are probably the most interesting and original contribution in this work -- They are, alone, worth this book's price.  In fact, Dawkins dares to question issues (like religious education for children) which are usually tacitly overlooked in our societies.  But this is what a scientist is morally bound to be: someone who is never tired of asking the very simple and basic questions, never takes something for granted, and always asks for rational arguments.  Even if you eventually disagree with Dawkins' opinions, every bit of this book is intellectually refreshing and stimulating.

0553208845|4.0|Curious about Buddhism?  Hermann Hesse's famous novel SIDDHARTHA is as engaging an entryway into eastern thought as you can find in any religious/eastern philosophy section of a bookstore, because it is both accessible and true to the system of beliefs set down so long ago.<br /><br />No, it is not a how-to manual complete with advice on meditation techniques, but it does clearly show how the protagonist's journey from the selfish hell of pleasing the body to the heavenly rewards of pleasing the soul can lead to oneness, completeness, and a unique form of happiness.<br /><br />In our material, self-absorbed times, the message is especially appropriate.  At its nadir, Siddhartha's life is comprised of the pursuit of wealth and power, the sexual pleasure of women, the lovely reveries of fine wines, expensive clothes, obedient servants, sweet-smelling perfumes, rich meat and fowl, the siren call of gambling, and the false entitlement that is idleness.  In short, he measures success in much the same way as many people in present-day capitalist nations.  It is when Siddhartha begins to feel owned by his possessions and paradoxically empty in the fullness of his successes that he desperately seeks answers.<br /><br />Although the novel is more telling than showing, readers should realize that its purpose is not so much in the plot as in its cautionary theme.  The ending, where Siddhartha's friend sees all life reflected in the protagonist's face, hammers home Hesse's appreciation of basic Buddhist concepts about life.  For the curious about and the adherents of Buddhism, it is a pleasing morality tale that may be predictable but is no less worth the journey.<br /><br />SIDDHARTHA is one of those books that serious readers should have under their belt at some point during their lifetimes, no matter where they are on their own personal spiritual journeys...

0618680004|4.0|This is a well-written, convincing book. Nevertheless one must keep in mind that this is a work of rhetoric, and it has all the weaknesses that good rhetoric has. Thus it highlights the ongoing search of atheists for a philosophically legitimate, but still convincing, way of getting their point across.<br /><br />As rhetoric, this book's real purpose is not to prove but to convince. A predictable centerpiece of the book is the argument that the evils perpetrated *in the name of* religion prove the evil *of* religion. Pretty standard stuff that goes down well with sympathetic readers, and skillfully wielded here. But Dawkins is a biologist, and \\"correlation equals causation\\" illogic is something the Scientific Method would never stand for. Indeed, Dawkins fails to convince us that a world without religion would be a world without religion's evils. The lengths to which Dawkins goes to excoriate religion rather suggest that an atheist world might rival a sectarian one in the capacity for hatred, if not for violence and exploitation.<br /><br />Like all good rhetoric, this book targets the reader's emotions. That is both its strength and its weakness. Dawkins argues for logic and reason, but rarely employs logic or reason in doing so. By trying to undermine religion using its own methods, Dawkins unwittingly confirms the supremacy of man's inherent emotionalism over his capacity for (or perhaps his willingness to) reason.<br /><br />It is no mistake that the works of anti-religion rhetoric are written by highly educated men who are trained to think logically and rationally about the world. But when even these men resort appeals to anger to convince, their arguments seem self-defeating.

B000O178BY|1.0|This is certainly a disputable album; in fact, it is a huge disappointment.  After reading some of the reviews already on Amazon, I am not unconvinced that Reznor himself (or any friends) wrote them given how unrealistic they are.  Is this the current state of music as it stands?  Have we lost that much aesthetic judgment, tact, and taste since the 1990s?  Are we that far gone?<br /><br />The Fragile was a fantastic album, and I have enjoyed Reznor since Pretty Hate Machine.  I can remember the caustic tone of voice throughout much of the Downward Spiral.  And I certainly appreciated the skillful composition of Fixed, though it was certainly no comparison to its predecessor, Broken.  I even thoroughly enjoyed Further Down the Spiral, especially if you were fortunate enough to grab the copy with Ruiner V.2 on it.<br /><br />Where does this leave Year Zero?  Year Zero belongs in Year Zero, a non-existent state.  This album should have never been made.  I was becoming more frustrated with Reznor as he began to become more political, but he has now finally reached the pinnacle of politicization and for that, he is forever lost.  This certainly does not mean that artists cannot inject their political views and attempt to infect those who listen.  Certainly not.  But when one becomes too seduced by the Siren of politics, one forgets about art, and this is where the problem lies.  It seems that Trent is now catering his politics to the same swine that he attacked with such vitriol in his earlier albums.<br /><br />After buying this album, I immediately started to listen to it.  Every Song is Exactly the Same.  I was willing to give this album away to a close friend who has been a connoisseur of good rock and a veteran of NIN, yet, he had already purchased the album.  Unfortunately, he corroborated my opinion and I realized I was not alone.  Reznor had mentioned some years back that he wanted to do Pop music, but it was going to be a different kind.  He terrified me when he uttered such words, but I thought perhaps he changed his mind.  It looks like this is exactly what he meant.<br /><br />Admittedly, this album does not deserve one star, perhaps 2, but certainly no more than 3.  I have only chosen 1 out of the disgust of so many people who felt it deserves 5. 0618680004|2.0|I must confess to picking up this book in some trepidation.  I am both a Ph.D. scientist as well as a Christian who has thought deeply and long about my faith, in the process reading books like this. The glowing reviews left me fearful that this book would leave me in a painful period of doubt and questioning.  Unfortunately, it seems that I didn't have to worry because I've found the book underwhelming and not all that challenging.  Unfortunately, I can only cover the most obvious things here.    First off, it is clear from the outset that Dr. Dawkins did not set forth to write a fair or balanced discussion of the belief in God.  In many cases the language used either shows a complete lack of understanding how others will react or else is purposely combative and insulting.  This immediately makes his views and reasoning suspect, which has been borne out by other reviewers who've showed how some quotes have been taken out of context.  I also find it interesting how Dr. Dawkins begins by quoting so much from Einstein, all except for Einstein's statement that, \\"There are people who say there is no God.  But what makes me really angry is that they quote me for support of such views.\\"  (Excerpt from Walter Isaacson's \\"Einstein\\" as published in Time Magazine, April 16, 2007).  I'm not saying that he inaccurately describes Einstein's beliefs, but leaving out that quote makes one wonder how others who were quoted would have felt about it.  As for his appeal to religion as being due to wishful thinking, he should be careful; that knife cuts both ways.  An atheist has his or her own reasons for hoping there isn't a God.  A careful reading will show some of Dawkin's.    Dr. Dawkins also incorrectly applies the anthropic principle, at least as far as I understand it.  The anthropic principle states that whatever the probability that intelligent life would develop capable that probability must be such that it would occur once because otherwise we wouldn't be here pondering thequestion.  If this is a correct understanding then the anthropic principle does not apply to the question of whether life is the result of God's action or rather to chance and natural selection.  The anthropic principle only applies if you assume that there was no active cause and therefore random chance is the only option.    Dr. Dawkins also attacks the NOMA proposition, i.e. that science and religion don't overlap because they don't deal with the same questions.  His argument is that miracles certainly fall under the magisterium of science because they are events that happen in the physical world.  However, what Dr. Dawkins fails to do is tell us what would have to happen in order for him to believe that he'd actually seen a miracle.  If one believes, as Dawkins seems to, that science does not allow for supernatural causes, then isn't he much more likely to think, \\"Well, that was something I don't understand, but I'm sure there's a perfectly natural explanation and I'm sure we'll understand it someday.\\"?  If science rules out supernatural causes by definition, and I agree that it does, then how can science ever be used to prove a miracle?           This same inability to step out of his paradigm, that paradigm being that there is nothing outside this universe and no options other than chance variation and natural selection, is what also makes his ultimate 747 argument unconvincing to anyone who does not share his initial beliefs as expressed in that paradigm.  And I've chosen the word \\"beliefs\\" on purpose because that's what they are. What Dr. Dawkins doesn't seem to understand is that if you don't subscribe to those beliefs, and Christians don't, then his argument is unconvincing and underwhelming.  To us, God is not in this box we call a universe.  All the mystics from all religions agree that God is not a thing or a being like anything we have even imagined.  That's not a cop out, it's simply a fact.  God doesn't \\"come from\\" anywhere, God has always existed without beginning or end.  Dawkins accuses intelligent design proponents for lacking imagination when they cite \\"irreducible complexity\\", but Dawkins also lacks in imagination; he seems unable to envision anything different from this universe.    Dawkins proposes that the 90% (or thereabouts) of people who believe in some kind of supernatural \\"God\\" are deluded.  But I would like to propose another hypothesis based on known science for the 10% who don't believe.  Many people who believe in God believe because they say they've experienced God somehow.  I'm not talking about miracles.  I'm talking about feeling a \\"presence\\" which has made a marked change in their lives.  We know that spirituality has a genetic component.  In a manner of speaking the ability to experience or sense God varies between people.  We know that this variation occurs with all our sense abilities.  Some people are color blind and so can't distinguish red and green.  Others can't taste certain tastes, like bitter.  I hypothesize that perhaps people like Dr. Dawkins are among that unfortunate minority of people who lack or have a very weak ability to sense God.  Unfortunately, this leads them to assert that God doesn't exist rather than considering that maybe the majority is able to \\"see\\" something that they cannot.

B000LXH0AE|5.0|If you consider yourself a big Will Ferrell fan, you might actually want to skip this movie. It is almost nothing like his usual style. Instead of playing a ridiculous buffoon through a series of cheap jokes, Ferrell is part of an intelligent, emotionally engaging film this time.    Harold Crick is an obsessively neat loner who works as an auditor for the IRS. Crick is very good at his job, which is a good thing, because that's nearly all he does. He has no social life and almost no outside interests, besides his unvarying routine. (Brush each tooth 35 times up and down, 35 side to side. Count the steps on the way to the bus.)    All of this changes when Crick starts hearing a voice narrating his actions. The voice describes exactly what he's doing as he's doing it. This understandably throws Crick off balance, and his work, among other things is negatively affected. Losing his cool, professional stance, he finds himself very attracted to the owner of a bakery who happens to be one of his auditees. (Hearing a voice carefully describe sexual fantasies about a woman present while trying to keep his mind on other things would be difficult for any man.)    Already worried, Crick becomes downright panicked when the voice announces his imminent death. Attempting to understand this strange experience, he consults with a professor of literature. (Professor Jules Hilbert is played by Dustin Hoffman. Hilbert is very reminiscent of his role as the existential detective in I Heart Huckabees.)    Paralleling all of this is the dilemma of Karen Eiffel, an author desperately trying to find an ending for her new novel. Her biggest problem is how exactly she's going to kill off Harold Crick.    Crick finally discovers exactly who is narrating his life (and death) and meets up with Eiffel. I won't ruin the ending, but I'll just say it's satisfying, if not perfect.    The script for this movie is fantastic, repeatedly flowing in directions that evade the audience's expectations. The entire cast puts in excellent performances. I, who am not a fan of Ferrell's usual style, was very pleasantly surprised by his genuine talent. Some people found the occasional CGI animations overlaid on the action distracting, but I thought they illustrated Crick's precise thought processes well. There are a few stray bits of plot that don't feel filled out very well, but nothing that detracts from the overall effect of this movie.    This is a great example of something we see far too rarely today: an intelligent, well-played comedy for grown-ups. If you're tired of watching Carrey, Stiller and Ferrell make fools of themselves in films like Zoolander or Dumb and Dumber, this is the perfect comedy for you.

0743496728|4.0|Possible Spoiler Alert...  It's hard to argue with this book's reviewers -- both below and in other publications: Jodi Picoult is a prolific author.  Her books cover timely, thought-provoking topics, and force us to realize that there is always another side to the story: Picoult illustrates to a fault the dangers inherent in making assumptions.  Picoult's novels are also thoroughly researched, and 19 Minutes is no exception.  Not only do we encounter vivid, credible portrayals of school shootings, bullying and the criminal justice system, but the question of happiness and the \\"happiness quotient\\" as an underlying theme reflect Picoult's familiarity with recent research and current courses at Harvard and other college campuses.      That said, 19 Minutes somehow fell short of my expectations, or perhaps, fell victim to hype.  The characters are strongly developed and evoke our sympathy; the plot is tightly woven.  But very often, the narrative feels tired and perfunctory, and lapses into navel-gazing.  The story clearly illustrates that characters' lives are unraveling, coming unglued, falling apart, will never be the same; no need to reinforce these images literally.  Also, several reviewers remark on the unbelievable plot twist surrounding Josie's character.  If you've read Picoult, you knew something was coming.  This device -- almost a reverse deus ex machina -- is the hallmark of her novels, and maybe this \\"unbelievability\\" is really a symptom that it's wearing thin.  Picoult foreshadows Josie's actions almost from the beginning: Matt's behavior becomes at times violent and possessive, and then turns to loving tenderness.  The denouement is disappointing, and hardly seems worthy of Josie's character; I felt more manipulated by this twist than anything.  Why not use the events to teach her the double lesson of \\"What price, popularity\\" and \\"When to stand up for yourself by NOT standing by your man\\"?      Picoult has written roughly one book per year since her first book was published.  19 Minutes was certainly entertaining, but in the end it was too predictable.  One wonders if she shouldn't slow the pace a bit.

0618680004|4.0|Dawkins's argument against religion rests on natural selection and the anthropic principle, but also on ridicule. Dawkins employs natural selection and the anthropic principle to successfully counter the current understanding of intelligent design; he employs ridicule and disbelief to counter the argument for God from personal experience. (He is gleefully abusive in his choice of adjectives.)  <br /> <br />Dawkins fortunately distinguishes between two kinds of religion: 1) rational religion, which is in theory an acceptable human endeavor until it is debunked by the anthropic principle and evolution; and 2) revealed religion, which is a self-debunking excercise in naivety. With regard to revealed religion, Dawkins essentially says: \\"show me a sign and I will believe.\\" <br /> <br />Dawkins offers an important and trenchant challenge to theology. I sympathize with this. I also think, however, that he is lacking in imagination. This is not only true when he is thinking about religion, but also when when he is thinking (or not thinking) about the theoretical problems with his own position. He speaks again and again about the importance of explanatory \\"cranes\\" as opposed to mere \\"skyhooks,\\" but at a certain point his scientism, I believe, itself becomes a skyhook. Reason requires that the validity of a system be determined from outside the system. He does a great job attacking theology from outside of theology (and we should be glad for the challenge), but he does not ever step outside his own system to reflect on its limits. This is what makes his work more polemic--perhaps even religious--than philosophical.  <br /> <br />And it IS a religious book, and not merely in the negative sense of being anti-religion. Consider the following.  <br /> <br />Dawkins seems to think that the difference between a believer and an atheist is that the believer's consciousness has not yet been \\"raised by Darwin\\" (145). This consciousness-raising through the prophet of science, Darwin, isalmost like a conversion. What Dawkins cannot understand (and he admits it) is someone who understands natural selection well, but still believes in God. About this person Dawkins says: \\"at an intellectual level, I suppose he understood natural selection. But perhaps you need to be steeped in natural selection, immersed in it, swim about in it, before you can truly appreciate its power\\" (117). \\"Steeped,\\" \\"Immersed,\\" \\"swim about\\"...like in a baptismal font? It's like going to Jesus Camp! The greatest Darwin-idolatry occurs on p. 146, where Dawkins says that opponents to the indemonstrable multi-/megaverse theory only oppose it because \\"they have not had their consciousness raised by natural selection.\\" Since when did natural selection become a law of cosmological physics? He is arguing aesthetically, just like religion. (In one of the calmer parts of the book he admits as much, but it does not seem to slow him down.) <br /> <br />When Dawkins focuses on what is central--i.e. the exact problems with the argument for God and the specific details of natural selection as a master narrative for everything--he is at his most engaging, convincing, and least angry. It would be nice if he stayed there. The more he polemicizes against modern-day religion, the more fundamentalist he himself becomes. The angriness he has toward the religious world is the very same angriness that he decries within the religious world. He has tolerance only for the things he likes, and he takes it upon himself to state authoritatively what humanity does and does not need. Where does this authority come from? If there is no transcendence, to what source does a moral stance appeal? If it is an immanent (Darwinian) source, how does Dawkins manage to assert the superiority of his worldview over that of other people? He delights in the prospect of religion being wiped from the earth. Naturally he does not recommend using guns, but does that make his emotion any less violent? I find the irony overwhelming. It is funny without being at all funny. He abominates religion, but he freely adopts its form. Atheists should be a community unto themselves with support centers, publications, meetings, and missionary efforts, and should feel free to deride all those who believe differently. Where did Dawkins learn this behavior? Possibly from British print culture, but more likely from religion itself. It is an old truth that apparent differences often conceal hidden similarities. <br /> <br />Finally, Dawkins's alternative explanation for religion is very far from being a full explanation. He explicitly says (to his credit) that he can only propose a *principle* by which religion came about (i.e. the \\"by-product\\" theory), but he cannot offer a specific account of it. Of course he takes a stab at offering a specific account, but it soon becomes evident that you can stick anything and everything into this \\"by-product\\" theory of religion. A bit of \\"innate dualistic thinking\\" here, a bit of \\"gullibility-as-a-means-of-securing-the-transmission-of-survival-skills\\" there, add a dash of \\"intentional judgments,\\" and about 10 (or 100? or 1000?) other things and voila: you have (by chance) religion! (All this is complicated by the fact that \\"memes\\" don't leave a clear record like genes do.) In short, there is no way to create a stable Darwinian narrative of religion. EVER. Theology may be full of theoretical problems, but natural selection cannot give a specific, definitive theory of religion. Personal taste suddenly plays a bigger role than it seemed. Are we inclined to \\"swim about\\" in Darwinian theory till all cows are black? Should we be so inclined?

0618680004|2.0|Richard Dawkins attempts to show that the non-existence of God is a scientifically demonstrable likelihood, and that any belief in God is irrational and detrimental to the well-being of humanity.    You don't need to read more than a few pages of this book to see that Richard Dawkins is not very well-schooled in philosophy or argumentative discourse. Dawkins quotes Pascal, Kant and Wittgenstein, but if he ever actually read any of their works he appears to have gained nothing from the experience. Dawkins does nothing to address the most serious philosophical and scientific objection to his book: that the question of whether or not God exists is not a scientific one. What's worse, Dawkins systematically lumps all religious beliefs together with fundamentalism. The result is that the book is full of straw man argumentation: attacks on creationism and literal interpretaion of scriptures. Things that few people of faith actually believe.    Even Dawkins' attempt to explain religious behavior through evolutionary psychology is thoroughly unconvincing. Part of the problem with the book is that some of the theories that Dawkins presents as \\"science\\", especially cutting-edge stuff like memes, string theory and evolutionary psychology, are bleeding-edge paradigms for which the empirical evidence is sketchy at best. It's no good arguing that there is no evidence of God, then offering up explanations for which the evidence is not much better.    The only good argument that Dawkins makes is that perhaps governments should stop supporting religious institutions with public money. That thesis is difficult to contest. Why should agnostics and atheists subsidize other people's beliefs?    The question of faith versus science is not new. In fact, one of the fundamental themes in modern philosophy from Descartes onward is how to reconcile the religious view of the world with the scientific one. The thing that people like Dawkins fail to understand is that the existence of God is a value judgement and not an empirical one. My favourite book on the subject is still Kierkegaard's [[ASIN:0691020280 The Sickness Unto Death]], but there are countless others that offer much more depth, insight and convincing argumentation than Dawkins' book.
